Only 36.3% of the 25,426 predicted genes for &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana &lt;/em&gt;are classified. For rice, only 20.4% of the 53,398 complete predictions, with both initial and terminal exons, could be classified.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F9-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F9.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F9-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F9.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F9.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Figure 9"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>

<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/529456" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F9-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Figure 9</span> <p id="p-67" class="first-child">Functional classification of rice genes, according to Gene Ontology Consortium, and assigned by homology to categorized<em>A. thaliana</em> genes. In this ontology, “biological process,” “cellular location,” and “molecular function” are treated as independent attributes. Only 36.3% of the 25,426 predicted genes for <em>A. thaliana </em>are classified. For rice, only 20.4% of the 53,398 complete predictions, with both initial and terminal exons, could be classified.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-40">We advise extreme caution in interpreting minor differences in functional classification between the predicted gene sets for rice and<em>A. thaliana</em>. With such a large fraction of the genes unclassified, intrinsic uncertainties in any classification scheme are amplified into artifactual differences. For example, the largest difference for InterPro was in signal transduction genes, but no notable difference was observed for Gene Ontology Consortium. Furthermore, focusing on small differences that had a high likelihood of being artifactual would distract from the major difference between rice and <em>A. thaliana</em>, which as we will show next, lies almost entirely in those genes with no functional classification.</p></div></div></div><div class="section" id="sec-22"><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h4>A. thaliana<em> comparisons.</em></h4><p id="p-41">In general, there are two ways to compare gene sets: through colinearity and homology. Colinearity of plant genomes has been studied extensively (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">84</a>, <a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">85</a>). For analyses done within a plant family, high degrees of colinearity have been consistently observed. Across the monocot-eudicot divide, with rice and <em>A. thaliana</em> as representative species, observed degrees of colinearity have been considerably lower (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">86–89</a>). For example, an analysis of a 340-kb segment on rice chromosome 2 identified 56 putative genes (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>). Homologs for 22 (39%) of them were identified in<em>A. thaliana</em>, but were distributed among 5 chromosomal segments, with several small-scale inversions. Another study of 126 rice BACs, totaling 20 Mb of sequence and with 3011 putative genes, identified homologs in <em>A. thaliana</em> for 1747 (58%) of these genes (<a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">89</a>). Typically, each 150-kb BAC mapped to three or more chromosomes. Notwithstanding the absence of colinearity, the finding that only half of the rice genes had a homolog in<em> A. thaliana</em> was unexpected. Although these analyses were based on predicted genes, which have not yet been confirmed, we do not believe that this was why so few rice genes had a homolog in <em>A. thaliana</em>, because a similar analysis was done with 27,294 unique ESTs from <em>Z. mays</em> (maize), and only 62% of the open reading frames had a homolog in<em> A. thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-90-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">90</a>).</p><p id="p-42">We focus exclusively on homology, rather than orthology, because extensive gene duplications in <em>A. thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-4-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>,<a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">91</a>) and rice make strict one-to-one pairing relations, the classic definition for orthology (<a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">92</a>), difficult to determine. A mere 35% of <em>A. thaliana</em> genes are unique and 37.4% belong to gene families with more than five members. Segmental duplications larger than 100 kb in size constitute 58% of the genome, and 17% of the genes are arranged in tandem arrays. In comparisons of rice with <em>A. thaliana</em>, and vice versa, we sought to compute the degree of homology in each direction, and the extent to which gene duplications in <em>A. thaliana</em> are replicated in rice when decomposed by functional classification. Even this modest objective was not easy to accomplish, because of unexpected complications introduced by the compositional gradients in rice.</p><p id="p-43"><em>Homology between monocots-eudicots.</em> The complete set of 25,426 annotated <em>A. thaliana</em> genes was downloaded from the<em>Arabidopsis</em> Information Resource Web site (<a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">93</a>) on 29 November 2001. As a control, 1441 proteins were downloaded from SwissProt (<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">94</a>) on the same day. The rice genes were restricted to the 53,398 predictions from FGeneSH with initial and terminal exons. We compared protein sequence to all six reading frames of the genome sequence by means of TblastN (<a id="xref-ref-36-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). Therefore, if the homology search failed, it would not be due to a gene being missing from the annotation of the target genome. The expectation value cutoff was set to 10<sup>−7</sup>. This was not a sensitive parameter, as most hits were either very good or very bad. What mattered was the “coverage rule.” We projected every hit back to the protein query, and unless a minimum fraction of the protein was covered, none of the hits were accepted. The hits had to occur in the same order in both the query and the target, and they all had to be in the same orientation. When a homolog spanned more than one scaffold, the coverage rule was imposed on each scaffold. From this rule, we estimated the number of homologs per gene, the extent of the homology, and the percentage amino acid identity (<a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">95</a>).</p><p id="p-44">The asymmetry in the monocot-eudicot analysis was striking (<a id="xref-fig-10-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F10">Fig. 10</a>). About 80.6% of <em>A. thaliana</em> genes had a homolog in rice. The mean extent of homology was 80.1% of the protein length, and there was 60.0% amino acid identity. If instead of the full set of annotated genes, we had used SwissProt genes, 94.9% of the genes would have had a homolog, across 86.7% of the protein length and at 72.9% amino acid identity. Presumably, there were more homologs in the SwissProt data because they were more biased toward highly conserved proteins. In contrast, only 49.4% of predicted rice genes had a homolog in <em>A. thaliana</em>. The mean extent of homology was 77.8% of the protein length, and there was 57.8% amino acid identity. For brevity, predicted rice genes with a homolog in <em>A. thaliana</em> are called WH genes, and those with no homologs are called NH genes. We identified two distinct problems in this analysis, both attributable to the compositional gradients in rice. One was the poor quality of the FGeneSH predictions for NH genes, and the other was related to the probability of identifying a TblastN hit even with a perfect gene annotation. We did use ESTs to confirm that NH genes were not false predictions, but first, we will discuss what we believe to be the true problems.</p><figure class="fig pos-float  odd figure figure--data" id="F10">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F10.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Distributions in extent of homology and maximum amino acid identity, for Arabidopsis-to-rice and rice-to-Arabidopsis comparisons. These values are based on a comparison of predicted protein sequence against all six reading frames of the target genome sequence." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-271354193" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Figure 10&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-68&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Distributions in extent of homology and maximum amino acid identity, for &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;-to-rice and rice-to-&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; comparisons. These values are based on a comparison of predicted protein sequence against all six reading frames of the target genome sequence.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F10-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F10.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F10-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F10.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F10.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Figure 10"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F10.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/529372" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F10-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Figure 10</span> <p id="p-68" class="first-child">Distributions in extent of homology and maximum amino acid identity, for <em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-rice and rice-to-<em>Arabidopsis</em> comparisons. These values are based on a comparison of predicted protein sequence against all six reading frames of the target genome sequence.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-45">We had previously observed that rice gene predictions were only 73.5% the size of <em>A. thaliana</em> gene predictions. This discrepancy is not due to the WH half of the rice genes. It is due to the NH half, which was on average 49.4% smaller than the WH half (<a id="xref-fig-11-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. 11</a>). To analyze the problem, we randomly sampled 3000 WH genes and 3000 NH genes, and applied the analyses of <a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a> to <a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7</a>. In general, WH genes resembled the “gold standard” based on alignment of cDNA to genomic sequence. NH genes exhibited a number of striking differences. First, the decreased coding region size was clearly due to a decrease in the number of exons, not to a decrease in the size of the exons. The GC-rich tail in NH gene exon distribution was twice as large as normal (<a id="xref-fig-11-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. 11</a>), suggesting that NH genes had more pronounced GC content gradients than either WH genes or those cDNAs retrieved from GenBank. It is plausible that FGeneSH performance would have faltered on NH genes, because NH genes did not resemble those genes on which FGeneSH was presumably trained. NH genes also had twice as many introns as normal in the 200- to 2000-bp range (<a id="xref-fig-11-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F11">Fig. 11</a>). This would be consistent with some of these missing exons being combined with their flanking introns. The preponderance of anomalous subminimal introns would be consistent with exon fragments being mistakenly called introns. However, NH genes could not be transposon sequences, because a 20-mer analysis confirmed that their constituent sequences were found in the genome at low copy numbers, much like WH and cDNA-derived genes.</p><figure class="fig pos-float  odd figure figure--data" id="F11">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F11.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Size distribution of predicted rice genes with a homolog (WH), and with no homolog (NH), in A. thaliana, plus exon GC content and intron size for a random sampling of 3000 NH genes. Gene size refers to the size of the predicted coding region." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-271354193" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Figure 11&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-69&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Size distribution of predicted rice genes with a homolog (WH), and with no homolog (NH), in &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;, plus exon GC content and intron size for a random sampling of 3000 NH genes. Gene size refers to the size of the predicted coding region.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F11-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F11.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F11-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F11.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F11.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Figure 11"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F11.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/529380" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F11-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Figure 11</span> <p id="p-69" class="first-child">Size distribution of predicted rice genes with a homolog (WH), and with no homolog (NH), in <em>A. thaliana</em>, plus exon GC content and intron size for a random sampling of 3000 NH genes. Gene size refers to the size of the predicted coding region.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-46">Although we do not entirely ascribe the small size of NH genes to a failure by FGeneSH to detect exons, it is likely that more exons were missed than for WH genes. Thus, it would be more difficult to identify a homolog for these genes in <em>A. thaliana</em>. However, even for experimentally derived gene sequences, like cDNAs, the probability of identifying a TblastN hit, as a function of the position, dropped precipitously near the 5′ end of the genes (<a id="xref-ref-52-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). Far from the end, the probability was about 90%, but within the first few hundred bases near the 5′ end, the probability dropped to less than 50%. This was another consequence of the compositional gradients in rice. The magnitude of the effect was unexpected. We had thought that selective constraints on coding sequences would have limited the number of amino acid changes, despite pressure from rising GC content. However, this was not the case. Homology searches were more likely to fail with the smaller NH genes because the problematic region was a larger fraction of their total length, and our “coverage rule” required that the TblastN hits cover a minimum fraction of the coding region. Even in the <em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-rice analysis, where the gene predictions were more reliable, 83.2%, 80.6%, 69.5%, and 48.5% of <em>A. thaliana</em> genes had a homolog in rice, for coverage rules of 0, 25, 50, and 75%. We had to use a relatively low coverage rule of 25%. Given the typical protein and protein domain sizes of 446 and 100 residues (<a id="xref-ref-96-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">96–98</a>), respectively, this was equivalent to one protein domain.</p><p id="p-47">Alternatively, what if the problem were due to scaffold size? Half of the NH genes were identified in a scaffold that was smaller than 7.1 kb. However, as a function of scaffold size, predicted coding regions for NH genes were almost always the same size. NH genes found in scaffolds greater than 7.1 kb were only 7% larger than those found in scaffolds less than 7.1 kb. Scaffold size could not have been responsible for the small size of the NH genes. Perhaps NH genes are not real genes at all. Are they even expressed? Looking back at our EST confirmation analysis, we found that 42.9% of WH genes were confirmed by a UniGene cluster, compared with 15.4% of NH genes. Assuming that all WH genes are real, this would imply that (15.4/42.9) × 100% = 35.9% of NH genes are real. However, if we adjust for their being 49.4% smaller than normal, attributing this size deficit to missed exons, then 72.7% of NH genes are real. Certainly, not every NH gene is real, but many are. To be conservative, we can adjust our gene count estimates by a factor of (0.494 + 0.727 × 0.506), resulting in a revised gene count of 46,022 to 55,615.</p><p id="p-48">Considering the relatively recent divergence between monocots and eudicots, 145 to 206 million years ago, it is surprising to find so many genes in rice with no homolog in <em>A. thaliana</em>. Even more intriguing, this absence of homology for NH genes extended to other sequenced organisms, including <em>D. melanogaster</em>,<em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em>, <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em>, and <em>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</em>. Although WH genes had a 30.5% probability of being homologous to at least one gene in one of these organisms, NH genes had a 2.4% probability. Hence, the major difference between rice and <em>A. thaliana</em> gene sets lies in that half of the predicted rice gene set with essentially no homologs in any organism, and whose functions are largely unclassifiable.</p><p id="p-49"><em>Duplication between monocots-eudicots.</em> Having established the major difference between the gene sets for rice and <em>A. thaliana</em>, we now consider the similarity. We had reported that 80.6% of the predicted <em>A. thaliana</em> genes, and 94.9% of the SwissProt genes, had a homolog in rice. The actual number is likely to be even higher, because the gradients kept us from identifying potential homologs for smaller genes. We know that, within <em>A. thaliana</em>, the genes are highly duplicated. Are these genes duplicated in the same manner when mapped to rice? As a proxy for the number of gene homologs within and between genomes, we used the “hits per gene,” as defined in the notes (<a id="xref-ref-95-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">95</a>). Considering that, in the <em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-rice comparison, we used a low coverage rule of 25% to compensate for the gradients, it was inevitable that we would experience more difficulty than usual in distinguishing between duplicated domains and duplicated genes. Thus, the number of hits per gene is an overestimate of the number of gene homologs.</p><p id="p-50">Comparing <em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-<em>Arabidopsis</em>(A2A), the mean and median hits per gene were 38.2 and 6.0, similar to the mean and median of 33.4 and 5.0 that we observed comparing <em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-rice (A2R) (<a id="xref-fig-12-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F12">Fig. 12</a>). That the A2R numbers would be slightly smaller makes sense, given the 145 to 206 million years of divergence. We further note that the means were large only because of a few outliers, some with up to 1000 hits. The identity of these outliers included protein kinase, cytochrome P450, putative disease resistance, and many “unknown” genes. It is difficult to draw any conclusions about the last category, but the others are highly duplicated gene families, which confirms that these outliers were not computational artifacts. The maximum amino acid identity was independent of the number of hits, but the minimum amino acid identity decreased with the number of hits, which would be consistent with an increasing occurrence of hits to ever larger families of related but divergent genes. Although the number of hits was dependent on the functional classification, it was similarly distributed among the different functional categories for A2R and A2A (<a id="xref-fig-13-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F13">Fig. 13</a>). Therefore, not only was it possible to identify a homolog in rice for almost every <em>A. thaliana</em> gene, but the patterns of gene duplication in one were largely replicated in the other.</p><figure class="fig pos-float  odd figure figure--data" id="F12">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F12.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Distributions in number of hits per gene and maximum-versus-minimum amino acid identity, forArabidopsis-to-rice andArabidopsis-to-Arabidopsis comparisons. &#x201C;Hits per gene&#x201D; is a proxy for the number of gene homologs, between and within genomes." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-271354193" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Figure 12&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-70&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Distributions in number of hits per gene and maximum-versus-minimum amino acid identity, for&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;-to-rice and&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;-to-&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; comparisons. &#x201C;Hits per gene&#x201D; is a proxy for the number of gene homologs, between and within genomes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F12-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F12.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F12-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F12.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">

  </figure><figure class="fig pos-float  odd figure figure--data" id="F13">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F13.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Distributions in the number of hits per gene, sorted according to Gene Ontology Consortium, forArabidopsis-to-rice andArabidopsis-to-Arabidopsis comparisons. This figure shows only the 36.3% of predicted A. thaliana genes that are classified." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-271354193" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Figure 13&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-71&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Distributions in the number of hits per gene, sorted according to Gene Ontology Consortium, for&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;-to-rice and&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;-to-&lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; comparisons. This figure shows only the 36.3% of predicted &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; genes that are classified.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F13-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F13.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F13-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F13.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F13.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Figure 13"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F13.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/529394" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F13-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Figure 13</span> <p id="p-71" class="first-child">Distributions in the number of hits per gene, sorted according to Gene Ontology Consortium, for<em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-rice and<em>Arabidopsis</em>-to-<em>Arabidopsis</em> comparisons. This figure shows only the 36.3% of predicted <em>A. thaliana</em> genes that are classified.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-51">The most parsimonious explanation is that the rice gene set is essentially a “superset” of the <em>A. thaliana</em> gene set. However, we are unable to say how many of these additional genes that are unique to rice are functionally novel, or merely unrecognizable, because of gradients in rice amino acid usage. It does seem unlikely that so many novel genes would arise within only 145 to 206 million years, and therefore, we suspect that a massive duplication event (or a series of duplication events) occurred, after which many of the rice genes were rendered unrecognizable by compositional gradients. Some may have been inactivated, and now exist only as pseudogenes. However, until we can compensate for the confounding effects of compositional gradients, we cannot explore the extent to which rice (<a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">99</a>) and many other plants, including <em>A. thaliana</em>, are hybrid (<a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">100</a>) or allopolyploid (<a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">101</a>, <a id="xref-ref-102-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">102</a>) in origin.</p></div></div></div><div class="section" id="sec-25"><div id="sec-26" class="subsection"><div id="sec-27" class="subsection"><h4>Rice polymorphisms.</h4><p id="p-52">Differences between subspecies or cultivars of rice must be described at two levels, gross and nucleotide. At the gross level, we found kilobase-sized regions of high similarity interspersed with kilobase-sized regions of no similarity. One such example is shown in<a id="xref-fig-14-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F14">Fig. 14</a>, which was based on a comparison of two overlapping BACs from <em>indica</em> and<em>japonica</em>. Every unalignable region coincided with a cluster of MDRs, traceable to length differences of 0.7 to 25 kb between the two source sequences, distributed in almost equal proportions between insertions and deletions. To the extent that BDRs could be identified, in roughly half of the unalignable regions, they belong to the class of nested retrotransposons that inhabit the intergenic regions between genes. This is another confirmation of the observation that genome sizes change rapidly in grasses (<a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">103</a>). On the basis of the available 259 kb of overlapping finished BAC sequences, from<em>Nipponbare</em> (<em>japonica</em>) and <em>GLA</em>(<em>indica</em>), all on rice chromosome 4, we would estimate that 16% of the <em>indica</em> and <em>japonica</em> genome is unalignable by this definition.</p><figure class="fig pos-float  odd figure figure--data" id="F14">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F14.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparison of GLA (indica) andNipponbare (japonica) BAC sequences (GenBank accession number AL442110 and AL606449, respectively). Exact 20-mer repeats are indicated by blue histogram bars, with bar heights proportional to copy number in 93-11 (indica). Sequence similarity is almost 100%, or unalignable and set to 20% by BLAST. Every unalignable region coincides with a cluster of MDRs, but RepeatMasker fails to identify a BDR in half of these regions." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-271354193" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Figure 14&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-72&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Comparison of &lt;em&gt;GLA&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;indica&lt;/em&gt;) and&lt;em&gt;Nipponbare&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;japonica&lt;/em&gt;) BAC sequences (GenBank accession number &lt;a class=&quot;external-ref external-ref-type-gen&quot; href=&quot;/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=AL442110&amp;atom=%2Fsci%2F296%2F5565%2F79.atom&quot;&gt;AL442110&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a class=&quot;external-ref external-ref-type-gen&quot; href=&quot;/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=AL606449&amp;atom=%2Fsci%2F296%2F5565%2F79.atom&quot;&gt;AL606449&lt;/a&gt;, respectively). Exact 20-mer repeats are indicated by blue histogram bars, with bar heights proportional to copy number in &lt;em&gt;93-11&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;indica)&lt;/em&gt;. Sequence similarity is almost 100%, or unalignable and set to 20% by BLAST. Every unalignable region coincides with a cluster of MDRs, but RepeatMasker fails to identify a BDR in half of these regions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F14-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F14.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F14-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F14.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F14.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Figure 14"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/296/5565/79/F14.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/529400" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F14-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Figure 14</span> <p id="p-72" class="first-child">Comparison of <em>GLA</em> (<em>indica</em>) and<em>Nipponbare</em> (<em>japonica</em>) BAC sequences (GenBank accession number <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=AL442110&amp;atom=%2Fsci%2F296%2F5565%2F79.atom">AL442110</a> and <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=AL606449&amp;atom=%2Fsci%2F296%2F5565%2F79.atom">AL606449</a>, respectively). Exact 20-mer repeats are indicated by blue histogram bars, with bar heights proportional to copy number in <em>93-11</em> (<em>indica)</em>. Sequence similarity is almost 100%, or unalignable and set to 20% by BLAST. Every unalignable region coincides with a cluster of MDRs, but RepeatMasker fails to identify a BDR in half of these regions.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-53">At the nucleotide level, excluding the unalignable regions, we define polymorphism rates for repeated and unique sequence, partitioned in single-base substitutions (single-nucleotide polymorphisms, SNPs) and insertion-deletion polymorphisms (InDels). By repeated sequence, we mean MDRs. Three different comparisons are shown in <a id="xref-table-wrap-4-1" class="xref-table" href="#T4">Table 4</a>. Two are based on the alignment of<em>93-11 </em>contigs to finished BAC sequences from<em>Nipponbare</em> (<em>japonica</em>) and <em>GLA</em>(<em>indica</em>), totaling 11.8 and 0.9 Mb, respectively. The other is a comparison of <em>93-11</em> and <em>PA64s </em>contigs. One might question the accuracy of a polymorphism rate based on rough draft sequence, particularly the low-coverage <em>PA64s</em> sequence. However, as we noted in our “quality assessments” section, most of the errors are in the small contigs and at the ends of the contigs. Thus, we restricted this analysis to contigs larger than 3 kb, with 500 bp trimmed off both ends. Overall, there was twice as much variation in the repeated regions as in the unique regions. Substitution rates were two to three times as large as InDel rates. Remarkably, there was very little difference among the three pairwise comparisons. For<em>93-11</em> to <em>PA64s</em>, averaged over repeated and unique regions, the SNP and InDel rates were 0.43 and 0.23%, respectively. Combining the SNP and InDel rates, we obtained an overall rate of 0.67%. Although the numbers are not exactly comparable, the measured polymorphism rate in maize was 0.96% (<a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">104</a>).</p><div class="table pos-float" id="T4"><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 4</span> <p id="p-76" class="first-child">Polymorphism rates relative to <em>93-11</em>(<em>indica</em>). Comparisons were made to finished BAC sequences from <em>GLA</em> (<em>indica</em>) and <em>Nipponbare</em>(<em>japonica</em>), as well as to <em>PA64s</em> contigs. Rates were computed for repeated and unique regions, in single-base substitutions (SNPs) and insertion-deletions (InDels). The numbers given for “unalignable” are a gross underestimate because RePS assemblies omit many of the fully masked reads that correspond to the unalignable regions of <a id="xref-fig-14-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F14">Fig. 14</a>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><div class="table-inline"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li><a href="/highwire/markup/529482/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li><li><a href="" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/529482/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_embed" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li></ul></div></div></div><p id="p-54">SNPs are useful in genetic mapping (<a id="xref-ref-105-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">105</a>), and are either directly applicable to phenotypes or indirectly applicable through linkage and association studies. Polymorphisms in the unique regions are particularly useful because, unlike those in the repeated regions, they are more reliably genotyped with existing high-throughput technologies, which always involve some sort of hybridization step. We expect that genome-wide SNP mapping in plants (<a id="xref-ref-106-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-106">106</a>) will become more popular as new technologies become available, especially as some are customized for plants (<a id="xref-ref-107-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-107">107</a>).</p></div></div></div><div class="section" id="sec-28"><div id="sec-29" class="subsection"><div id="sec-30" class="subsection"><h4>Concluding remarks.</h4><p id="p-55">In the initial annotation of the human genome (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>,<a id="xref-ref-8-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>), alternative splicing was proposed as a method by which protein diversity could be generated from the surprisingly small number of genes that were identified. The idea that there is extensive alternative splicing in human genes has been supported by analyses of EST data (<a id="xref-ref-108-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-108">108–112</a>). Alternative splicing is often associated with the exon recognition model (<a id="xref-ref-113-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-113">113</a>) of pre-mRNA splicing. Exon recognition is facilitated by exonic splicing enhancers—short, degenerate sequences located in the exons that are recognized by a multitude of RNA binding factors (<a id="xref-ref-114-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-114">114</a>,<a id="xref-ref-115-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-115">115</a>). Because it is the exons that are recognized by the splicing machinery, the intron sequence content is less critical, and transposon insertions into the intron are more readily tolerated. Thus, the preponderance of large transposons-filled introns in the human genome is consistent with extensive alternative splicing.</p><p id="p-56">The presence of relatively few transposons inside plant introns suggests that exon recognition is not a common process for plant genes. Indeed, exonic splicing enhancers have yet to be identified in plants (<a id="xref-ref-116-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-116">116</a>). The corollary is that there should be relatively little alternative splicing in plant genes. Analysis of the EST data confirms that <em>A. thaliana</em> has substantially less alternative splicing than vertebrates or invertebrates (<a id="xref-ref-117-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-117">117</a>). However, protein diversity must be generated for the organism to evolve. Our analysis has demonstrated extensive gene duplications in rice and <em>A. thaliana</em>, which are highly correlated with each other when decomposed by functional classification. The conclusion is that protein diversity in plants is generated primarily through gene duplications, whereas in vertebrates, it is generated through gene duplications and alternative splicing. This would explain why rice has so many genes. However, as a method of generating protein diversity, gene duplications come at the cost of an increase in transcriptional noise (<a id="xref-ref-118-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">118</a>). Perhaps, at some level of complexity, alternative splicing becomes preferred.</p><p id="p-57">Looking to the future, we intend to improve our draft sequence by adding more reads from large-insert clones, filling any gaps that are likely to contain genes, and integrating the resultant sequence with existing physical and genetic maps. The large-insert clones are necessary to correctly assemble across the large repeat clusters that are sprinkled throughout the rice genome. Until then, the BAC-end sequences (<a id="xref-ref-119-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-119">119</a>) may not be useful because they are too large to bridge adjacent contigs, and instead skip intervening contigs, resulting in a morass of interleaving scaffolds. One should also be wary of large-scale differences between <em>indica </em>and<em>japonica</em>. In any event, the final assembly will be made freely available to the research community. We will then apply the experiences gained from the rice genome project to other agriculturally important crops, including <em>Z. mays</em> (maize) and <em>T. aestivum</em> (wheat).</p></div></div></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><ul><li class="fn" id="fn-1"><p id="p-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-1-1">↵</a><span class="fn-label">*</span> These authors contributed equally to this work.</p></li><li class="fn-corresp" id="fn-2"><p id="p-2"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-2-1">↵</a><span class="fn-label">†</span> To whom correspondence should be addressed. E-mail: <span class="em-link"><span class="em-addr">hyang{at}genomics.org.cn</span></span>.</p></li></ul></div><div class="section ref-list" id="ref-list-1"><h2>REFERENCES AND NOTES</h2><ol class="cit-list"><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-1-1" title="View reference 1 in text" id="ref-1">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-journal" id="cit-296.5565.79.1" data-doi="10.1016/S1369-5266(99)00047-3"><div class="cit-metadata"><ol class="cit-auth-list"><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">T.</span> <span class="cit-name-surname">Sasaki</span></span>, </li><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">B.</span> <span class="cit-name-surname">Burr</span></span></li></ol><cite>, <abbr class="cit-jnl-abbrev">Curr. id="ref-28">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-other" id="cit-296.5565.79.28"><div class="cit-metadata unstructured">For the plasmid shotgun libraries, a DNA isolation protocol was modified from Sambrook and Russell (29). Fresh leaves at the seeding stage were ground in liquid nitrogen before complete lysis (30). Purified high–molecular weight genomic DNA was sonicated and sized on agarose gels, selecting for fragments of size 1.5 to 3.0 kb. QIAEX Gel Extraction Kit (QIAGEN) was used to purify DNA from the gel slices. Genomic fragments were ligated to Sma I–linearized pUC18 plasmids and transformed into DH10B-competent cells by electroporation.</div><div class="cit-extra"></div></div></li><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-28-1" title="View reference 29 in text" id="ref-29">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-other" id="cit-296.5565.79.29"><div class="cit-metadata unstructured">J. Biol.</abbr> <span class="cit-vol">3</span> <span class="cit-pub-date">2001</span> <span class="cit-fpage">47</span>; <a href="http://www.expasy.com">http://www.expasy.com</a>.</cite></div><div class="cit-extra"><a href="{openurl}?query=rft.jtitle%253DCurr.%2BIss.%2BMol.%2BBiol.%26rft.volume%253D3%26rft.spage%253D47%26rft.atitle%253DCURR%2BISS%2BMOL%2BBIOL%26rft.genre%253Darticle%26rft_val_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Ajournal%26ctx_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ctx_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Actx" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a></div></div><div class="cit ref-cit ref-other" id="cit-296.5565.79.100"><div class="cit-metadata unstructured">; <a href="http://www.expasy.com">http://www.expasy.com</a>.</div><div class="cit-extra"></div></div></li><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-95-1" title="View reference 95 in text" id="ref-95">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-other" id="cit-296.5565.79.101"><div class="cit-metadata unstructured">For each protein query, we created an array with one element for each amino acid position. Blast_hits() recorded the number of times that each position was covered by a TblastN hit. Each hit had associated with it a score for the percentage of identically matched amino acids. AA_identity() recorded the maximum and minimum score at each position, across all TblastN hits. “Extent of hit,” quoted as a percentage of the protein length, is the number of nonzero elements in Blast_hits(). “AA identity” and “hits per gene” are the median values of AA_identity() and Blast_hits(), computed over positions with one or more hits. We used the median, instead of the mean, to minimize the likelihood of counting a highly duplicated domain when the entire protein is not duplicated.</div><div class="cit-extra"></div></div></li><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-96-1" title="View reference 96 in text" id="ref-96">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-journal" id="cit-296.5565.79.102" data-doi="10.1093/protein/8.6.513"><div class="cit-metadata"><ol class="cit-auth-list"><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">S.
